<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">List of Sega Genesis games</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script><div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sega-Genesis-Mk2-6button.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2960" data-file-width="5760" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Sega-Genesis-Mk2-6button.jpg/250px-Sega-Genesis-Mk2-6button.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Sega-Genesis-Mk2-6button.jpg/375px-Sega-Genesis-Mk2-6button.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Sega-Genesis-Mk2-6button.jpg/500px-Sega-Genesis-Mk2-6button.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sega-Genesis-Mk2-6button.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Sega Genesis model 2 (North America)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Sega_Genesis" title="Sega Genesis">Sega Genesis</a>, known as Mega Drive <span style="font-weight: normal">(<a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a>: <span class="t_nihongo_kanji" lang="ja" xml:lang="ja">メガドライブ</span><span class="t_nihongo_comma" style="display:none">,</span> <a href="/wiki/Hepburn_romanization" title="Hepburn romanization">Hepburn</a>: <i>Mega Doraibu</i><sup class="t_nihongo_help noprint"><a href="/wiki/Help:Installing_Japanese_character_sets" title="Help:Installing Japanese character sets"><span class="t_nihongo_icon" style="color: #00e; font: bold 80% sans-serif; text-decoration: none; padding: 0 .1em;">?</span></a></sup>)</span> in most regions outside North America, is a <a href="/wiki/16-bit" title="16-bit">16-bit</a> <a href="/wiki/Video_game_console" title="Video game console">video game console</a> that was developed and sold by <a href="/wiki/Sega" title="Sega">Sega Enterprises, Ltd.</a> First released in Japan in 1988 and in North America in 1989, the Genesis is Sega's third console and the successor to the <a href="/wiki/Master_System" title="Master System">Master System</a>. The system supports a library of more than 700 games created both by Sega and a wide array of third-party publishers and delivered on <a href="/wiki/ROM_cartridge" title="ROM cartridge">ROM cartridges</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SegaSpecs_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-SegaSpecs-1">[1]</a></sup> It can also play <a href="/wiki/List_of_Master_System_games" title="List of Master System games">the complete library of Master System games</a> when the separately sold Power Base Converter is installed. The Genesis also benefited from numerous <a href="/wiki/Video_game_accessory" title="Video game accessory">peripherals</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Sega_CD" title="Sega CD">Sega CD</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sega_32X" title="Sega 32X">Sega 32X</a>, several network services, and multiple first-party and third-party variations of the console that focused on extending its functionality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllgameGenesis_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-AllgameGenesis-2">[2]</a></sup> The console and its games continue to be popular among fans, collectors, video game music fans, and emulation enthusiasts. Licensed third party re-releases of the console are still being produced,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blaze_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blaze-3">[3]</a></sup> and several <a href="/wiki/Indie_game" title="Indie game">indie game</a> developers continue to produce games for it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-retronintendolife_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-retronintendolife-4">[4]</a></sup> Many games have also been re-released in compilations for newer consoles and offered for download on various digital distribution services, such as <a href="/wiki/Virtual_Console" title="Virtual Console">Virtual Console</a>, <a href="/wiki/Xbox_Live_Arcade" title="Xbox Live Arcade">Xbox Live Arcade</a>, <a href="/wiki/PlayStation_Network" title="PlayStation Network">PlayStation Network</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Steam_(software)" title="Steam (software)">Steam</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wii_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wii-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kotaku1_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kotaku1-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kotaku3_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kotaku3-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Steam_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Steam-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Genesis library was initially modest, but eventually grew to contain games to appeal to all types of players. The initial pack-in title was <i><a href="/wiki/Altered_Beast" title="Altered Beast">Altered Beast</a></i>, which was later replaced with <i><a href="/wiki/Sonic_the_Hedgehog_(1991_video_game)" title="Sonic the Hedgehog (1991 video game)">Sonic the Hedgehog</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Retroinspection_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Retroinspection-9">[9]</a></sup> Top sellers included <i>Sonic the Hedgehog</i>, its sequel <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sonic_the_Hedgehog_2_(16-bit_video_game)" title="Sonic the Hedgehog 2 (16-bit video game)">Sonic the Hedgehog 2</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Disney%27s_Aladdin_(Virgin_Games)" title="Disney's Aladdin (Virgin Games)">Disney's Aladdin</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IGNnumbers_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-IGNnumbers-10">[10]</a></sup> During development for the console, Sega Enterprises in Japan focused on developing <a href="/wiki/Action_game" title="Action game">action games</a> while Sega of America was tasked with developing <a href="/wiki/Sports_game" title="Sports game">sports games</a>. A large part of the appeal of the Genesis library during the console's lifetime was the <a href="/wiki/Arcade_game" title="Arcade game">arcade</a>-based experience of its games, as well as more difficult entries such as <i><a href="/wiki/Ecco_the_Dolphin" title="Ecco the Dolphin">Ecco the Dolphin</a></i> and sports games such as <i><a href="/wiki/Joe_Montana_Football" title="Joe Montana Football">Joe Montana Football</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Retroinspection_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Retroinspection-9">[9]</a></sup> Compared to its competition, Sega advertised to an older audience by hosting more mature games, including the uncensored version of <i><a href="/wiki/Mortal_Kombat_(1992_video_game)" title="Mortal Kombat (1992 video game)">Mortal Kombat</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Retroinspection_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-Retroinspection-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>This is an alphabetical list of released games for the Genesis. Titles listed do not include <a href="/wiki/List_of_Sega_CD_games" title="List of Sega CD games">releases for the Sega CD</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Sega_32X_games" title="List of Sega 32X games">Sega 32X</a> add-ons. Included in this list are titles not licensed by Sega, including releases in Taiwan by several developers such as <a href="/wiki/Gamtec" title="Gamtec">Gamtec</a>, as well as releases by <a href="/wiki/Accolade_(game_company)" title="Accolade (game company)">Accolade</a> before being licensed following the events of <i><a href="/wiki/Sega_v._Accolade" title="Sega v. Accolade">Sega v. Accolade</a></i>. This list also includes titles developed by unlicensed third-party developers after the discontinuation of the Genesis, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Pier_Solar_and_the_Great_Architects" title="Pier Solar and the Great Architects">Pier Solar and the Great Architects</a></i>.</p>
<p>A few games were only released exclusively on the <a href="/wiki/Sega_Channel" title="Sega Channel">Sega Channel</a> subscription service, which was active from 1994 to 1998, in the US. This means that, whilst cartridges were officially released for use on PAL and Japanese consoles, they were unavailable physically in the US. While few games were released this way, some of them are considered to be staples in the Genesis library, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Pulseman" title="Pulseman">Pulseman</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Mega_Man:_The_Wily_Wars" title="Mega Man: The Wily Wars">Mega Man: The Wily Wars</a></i>.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sega_Meganet_titles">Sega Meganet titles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=List_of_Sega_Genesis_games&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Sega Meganet titles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sega_Meganet" title="Sega Meganet">Sega Meganet</a>, also known as the Net Work System, was an online service for the Mega Drive in Japan. Utilizing <a href="/wiki/Dial-up_Internet_access" title="Dial-up Internet access">dial-up Internet access</a>, Meganet was Sega's first <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">online</a> <a href="/wiki/Multiplayer_game" title="Multiplayer game">multiplayer gaming</a> service, and functioned on a <a href="/wiki/Pay_to_play" title="Pay to play">pay to play</a> basis. The system functioned through the use of a peripheral called the Mega Modem and offered several unique titles that could be downloaded, and a few could be played competitively with friends. In addition, it shared technology and equipment with more serious services such as the Mega Anser, used for banking purposes. Though the system was announced for North America under the rebranded name "Tele-Genesis", it was never released for that region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Disconnected_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Disconnected-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Meganet service utilized its own library of titles, independent of the Genesis library. Most of these games never received a cartridge release; however, <i><a href="/wiki/Columns_(video_game)" title="Columns (video game)">Columns</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Flicky" title="Flicky">Flicky</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Fatal_Labyrinth" title="Fatal Labyrinth">Fatal Labyrinth</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Teddy_Boy_Blues" title="Teddy Boy Blues">Teddy Boy Blues</a></i> each later saw cartridge versions. Several Meganet games would also later appear in <i>Game no Kanzume vol. 2</i>, released for the <a href="/wiki/Sega_CD" title="Sega CD">Mega-CD</a> exclusively in Japan. Most games for the service were small, at around 128kB per game, due to the limits of Internet connection speeds at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TeddyBoy_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-TeddyBoy-30">[30]</a></sup> Downloads were estimated to take about five to eight minutes to complete.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Disconnected_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Disconnected-29">[29]</a></sup> All of the Meganet games were available through the Sega Game Library, accessed through the Meganet modem. Due to issues with long-distance charges through the use of telephone lines, as well as seconds of lag time between commands, only two games featured competitive play: <i><a href="/wiki/Tel-Tel_Stadium" title="Tel-Tel Stadium">Tel-Tel Stadium</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Tel-Tel_Mahjong" title="Tel-Tel Mahjong">Tel-Tel Mahjong</a></i>, with the remainder of the games available for single players via download. Due to Sega's reluctance to commit to releasing the service in North America, <a href="/wiki/Video_game_developer" title="Video game developer">third-party developers</a> in that region were unwilling to invest in developing games specifically for Meganet. This resulted in a low number of titles created for the service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Disconnected_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-Disconnected-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>The following list contains all of the titles released for the Meganet service. All titles in this list were released in Japan only.</p>
